Reconciliation Is 
Believed Unlikely 

Quezon Says He Wants To 
Start Election Drive . 
Next Few Days 

The existence a ‘hloc*’ in the 
senate, aiming to reconcile Senate 
Quezon and Senator Os- 
nieha so 'th^t'^TKey may again join 
in one political faction, was finally 
discredited by several majority and^ 
minority senators, who insist that aj 
coalition is an impossibility, at least ( 
for some time to come. ' i 

It was generaFy admitted in the ’ 
senate that Senator Jose M. Veloso 
has always been for the “reunion’^ 
of Messrs. Quezon and Osmeha, but ’ 
under the present circumstances the ■ 
chances of having them join within j 
I one political faction are slim. There | 
is certainty that the two 
fighting factidns will be united on 
national questions but not on party 
lines. 

Pi’esident Quezon^ on being ask- 1 
ed of the existence of such a bloc, 
said he did not know anything 
about, it but that he would be will- 
ing to reconcile with anyone or. to 
fight. 

The senate president to’d news- 
papermen that he wanted to start 
his election carapaigning in variou,s 
places, and that he would consider 
the question of naming a candiijate 
for representative to run " against 
Representative Francisco Varona in 
the north district of Manila, 
not interested in suspending the .^c 
President ' Quezon' said ' that he is 
tioiis, and that the sooner the elec- 
tions ,come the better. He is confid- 
ent that the election victory will be 
with' the new majority.

mittee to investigate the Philippines 
whether there is harmony over the 
national questions prevailing.

Another Trip 

Will Work For Improfements 
!n Economic Provisions 

Manila, May 3. — Senate Presi- 
dent Manuel L. Quezoirannounced 
today at the banquet given in 
his honor by augar planters that 
in August, 1934, he will go back 
to the United States to work for 
improvements of the Tydings- 
McDuffie Independence Law, es- 
pecially its economic provisions 
I whicluwonid endanger the eco- 
‘nomic stability of the Philippines.' 
iHe pointed out that the Con- 
jgress would be willing to enter- 
itain any petition for improve- 
' ment of the economic provisions 
of the act. It is likely that the 
senate president will fight the su- 
gar limitation bill and the excise 
tax on Philippine coconut oil. 

It was learned today that in 
view of the fixed quota for Phi- 
lippine sugar entering the United 
States duty free, there will be 
about 40lr,o6o tons surplus this 
year, and in 1935 the surplus will 
increase to 800,000 tons if no 
[means are devi.sed to sell all oi; 
i sugar.

The “antis” wanted to make the 
day of fQ^uezpnJs arrival a holiday. 
But on second thought, they didn't 
because the government employees 
could not be compelled to attend the 
pier reception. As it was, depart- 
ment orders were given (except in 
bureaus under the Gov- Gen.) for 
all employees to be present at the* 
pier at 7:00 a.m. sharp for a roll 
call. Last year, when the “antis” 
hold a parade during the opening 
of the Legislature, similar depart- 
ment orders were also issued, com- 
pelling government employees to 
march in the parade. 

The poor employees also had to 
pay Pl.OO each for a ticket to the 
banquet in honor of Quezon last 
Monday. They nicked on those who 
receive above fSO.OO only, however. 

The “anti” employees took these 
as unpleasant obhgations. The 
. ‘ ‘ pro ’ ’ employees, ' who comprise 
two-thirds of the men in the gov- 
ernment service, decry them as ty- 
ranny. But they know the penalty, 
so life goes on '

PRESIDENT QUEZON SHOT BY MRS. MELENGIO, LOCAL 

- WEEKLY CLAIMS 



If SAKDAL’S In^innatiuns Aren’t Tr je, Paper Must Be Prosecuted To Vin- 
dicate Senate President and Ag:uinaldo Family 

J^EPRODUCED aboVe-is part of the issue of SAKDAL for April 7 last which, 
although innocent-looking and innocently worded, insinuates more than 
enough to cast, reflection on the name, honor, and dignity of Senate Pres- , 
ident Quezon and the Aguinaldo family. 

The article talks of rumors reported to be widely circulated in Cavite 
regarding an incident in wh’ch Mrs. Melencio is said to have shot President 
Quezon, causing the latter to be confined in a New York hospital. The 
SAKDAL does not state the reason for the alleged shooting, but between 
the lines,' the reader can read just what 'that paper tries to convey. 

Considering the grave nature of the insinuation, and the parties af- 
fected such publication should not be indeed allowed to pass without 
clarification. More than the mere names of Mr. Quezon and General 
Aguinaldo are involved. The honor of the country itself which has for 
these two men as national leaders is likewise affected. President Quezon 
cannot afford to dismiss this serious charge with the usual “I don't take 
it seriously" gesture. 

Would President Quezon allow the people to beheve that their national 
leader could be capable of violating one of the most sacred of human 
and Divine laws, by attempting on the honor of a married woman and 
daughter of a friend at that? Certainly, he would hot. Only one alter- 
native is thus open to him. If SAKDAL’S imputations are not true, he 
should take the case to court and vindicate his name and that of the 
Aguinaldo family against whom the unpleasant aspersions have been ca.st 
by the publication. •'

Daia. j^TqT 7 iml lyu^ ii. Jresidente del Senado de Pilipinas, 

tendra todos los del^toTqtie sus adversarios quieran apuntarle, ; 
pero ncsotros decimos que el es un hombre admirable. U) es por 
6u coraje, por su franqueza y porque, cuando se empena en una , 
empresa, se mete de cabeza en ella, jugando siempre el todo ' 
por el’ todo, como lo ha demostrado muchas veces, particulai- . 
mente en su- ultimo viaje a Estados Unidos, en medio de lasi 
mis desfavorables circunstancias; y un hombre que precede asi, . 
ha de conquistar siempre el corazon de todos. j 

El mas reciente arranque de Quezon puede verse en el 
Certificado que ha presentado a las autoridades correspondSon-* 
tes, en relacion con su candidatura al mismo puesto de Sen'idor^ 
que viene ocupando desde el aho 1916: Quezon declara, bajo^U;:^ 
ramento, que no puede especificar los gastos para su campsP 
na, — como lo exige la Ley Electoral, — por la sencilla razon de, 
que 61 no piensa gastar ni un solo centavo, para el triunfo de> 
tu candidatura. Original. jOriginalisimo! Como que nadie lo ha., 
hecho hasta ahora. Por el contrario, la practica corriente es 
gastar rnucho dinero, pagando munidores y comprando votes, 
aunque ocultando despues la verdadera cantidad que se ha gas-^^ 
tado, a fin de no incuiTir en la penalidad marcada por la ley. ■ 
Creemos que la actitiid adbptada ahora por el Presidente' 
Quezon, sobre ser noble y caballercsca, es eminentemente educa»‘ 
tiva para nuestro pueblo; por esto, la aplaudimos, con toda la, 
fuerza de que :Bomos capaces. Gracias al dinero, insignes botara- 
tes logran encaramarse a los mas elevaaos puestos electivos, man- 
teni6ndose luego alii por ahos y ahos. Por igual razon, hombres 
cle grande vaha, pero desprovistos de fondos, quedan relegado: 
al ostracismo, sin tener oportunidad de servir al pais con suf: 
luces y habilidades. Al paso que van las cosas, la politica no esi 
otra cesa sino una tienda asquerosa, donde el factor iinico es eh 
voto pagado, y lo demas no vale nada. Asi se ven tantos grafts 
y grafters en el gobiemo, porque quien ha logrado su exaltacion 
politica mediante el dinero, es natural qus busque ocasion de re- 
cuperar despues lo gastado. . .

V,tritOySenato»M eh que Quezon se presenta candidate. El mundoj

sin necesidaci/d^‘\situar fondos de campaha? Pocos dias faltan 
para que sepfhros la contestacion a esta pregunta.

President Manuel L. Quezon will 
preside over the openinf^&SVemo- 
nies of the Par Eastern Olympic 
games to be held at the baseball 
stadium of the Rizal Memorial 
fields tomorrow afternoon at two 
o’clock p. m. All foreign athletes 
and officials as A^ell as the Filipino 
contingent will take part in the af- 
^fair. The different delegations 
will assemble at the La Salle Col- 
lege and at /2 o’clock p. m. will 
start for the Rizal grounds. The 
imost attractive feature in store for 
!the public will be the release of 
several pigeons with the colors of 
the different countries represent- 
3 d in the Olympic games. The Con- 
stabulary band will supply the mu- 
sic and will accompany the chorus 
which will sing 'Thilippines, My 
Philippines.” 

The entertainment committee 
took the foreign athletes and their 
officials on a sightseeing tour 
around the city yesterday morn- 
ing from 8 to 11 o’clock, a. m. 
Whereas the purpo.se of the tour 
was to show the city to the^^of- 
eign visitors, incidentally it WfcfT 
hoped that the affair will help to 
boost the Olympic games which 
will ^ .start tomorrow afternoon. 
The delegations were taken to the 
Walled City, around the Dewey 
Boulevard and to the other inter- 
esting sites within Manila.

FALLING REVENUES CHIEF 
CAUSE OF EXECUTIVE 
DRASTIC STEP 



(Special to the Advertiser) 

MANILA, May 10.— The execu- 
tive departments of the p;overnment 
are not recommending the introduc- 
tion of bills providing for funds for 
public works thus eliminating the 
pork barrel. This policy is pursued 
in view of the lowering of revenues 
as a result of the excise tax and 
other reactionary measures against 
the. islands by the United States 
«ongi*ess. 

Consistent with this policy, the 
Finance department refused to cer- 
tify pre-election public works. It 
was revealed that the main reason 
behind this action of the depart- 
ment was that the Anti candidates 
are hoping to win the elections by 
capitalizing on public works. Re- 1 
liable reports have it that President 
Quezon^ men won't run unless thQ 
workf funds were released. 
They prefer to withdraw to risk- 
ing their candidacies in a sure and 
certain defeat. | 

Taking cognizance of this attiudej 
of the Antis, the finance depart-! 
ment is said to have taken the ne- 
cessary precautron of withholding 
the public works funds to the end 
that they may not be usj^d for par- 
tisan ends. . / J 

Observers point to the experietieo 
in the province of Cebu where the 
pay roll is alleged to be almost to- 
tally filled with anti names. The 
atep the finance department has tak- 
en in the direction of .sound gov- 
ernment has cheered many disinte- 
rested persons.

If there is any opportunity to improve tlie economic 
provisions of the Independence Law to our advantage, we 

should make the most of it. 



More Delusions 

— j~ == 



If Presitlent Quezon, in the 
event that he is retained in 
power, should leave for thej 
United Stote.s to seek for ns 
that advantage, we would wish him all success. 

If the members of Congress who have been invited to 
\isit these islands are so powerful and influential that they 
'Will be able to induce the American government to grant 
, . us that concession, we shall all be grateful. 

But not because we have this wish to be so served by 
■the American government, should we encourage our people 
to have delusions about our economic future. 

The one great reality before the country today is a mat- 
ter of cause and effect. The cause is the comiiig of indep- 
endence, and the effect is the setback in our economic life. 

Confidence in our future should be builded upon' that 
t .reajity. It offers the only basis for security because what 
we shall construct upon it as an economic set-up will be a 
pei'manent unit. What we'may have by way of concessions 
under the United States tariff laws wll be, on the other 
hand, a temporai’y blessing. Governor General Mui-phy, be 
it recalled, has called that blessing a fool’s paradise.

AS WE 00 TO PRESS 



. Legtslature Accepts 
Independence Law 

Acting corifcurrently at a joint 
session Tuesday noon, the P. I. 
legislative^ body unanimously 
accepted the ,Tydings-McDuffie 
independence, law, thereafter 
informing tjie governor-general 
of the acti<m taken so that he 
might transmit the news to the 
'U. S. congress, President Roose- 
velt. The g.-g. shortly after 
noon advised the Washington 
(D. C.) authorities accordingly. 
Passage of the resolution was 
carried without a hitch and 
was witnessed by a packed gal- 
lery. Senate President .Quezon, 
whose arrival at about 11 o’clock 
was greeted with applause, 
opened the session. Rep. Del- 
gado stood up, was recognized 
by the chair. He moved that the 
liberty law be accepted through 
a concurrent resolution pre- 
pared by Senate President Que- 
zon, majority leader, and ac- 
cepted by the minority, through 
Senator Osmena, last Monday. 
This was immediately seconded 
■by Senator Clarin, senate presi- 
dent tempore. President 

Quezon then called for the 
vote. The secretaries went 
through the polls, reading 
names and jotting down the 

otes. There was not one nay.

Cebu Admirers Of Leader Want 
Him To Campaign In Province 

(Special UNION .Service) 

CEBU, Cebu, May 12. — The news that Senate President Quezon 
has definitely decided not to come to Cebu and help the "Antis” of 
this province has caused threat disappointment <o many of his ad* 

1 mirers here. 

It is said that Ihosident Quezon made up his mind not to come 
to Cebu for the reason that the ‘‘Pros” are sure to be defeated. But 
' ‘‘Pros'" are opcid>* talking here to the effect that President Quezon 
is afraid to invade the bailiwick of Osmeha. 

Wlj^atever the excuse may be, many admirers of Quezon 
explain, there seems to be no justification for the plan of the senate 
head not to extend his “Anti" campaign to Cebu. If there is any 
province which he should and must invade it is Cebu for the reason 
that here Osmeha, who should be repudiated by the elecioraie, is 
seeking re-election. 

There is. an opinion here that Quezon's failure to come may be' 
interpreted to mean that he is not sincere in his attempt to rout out 
the “Pros” for their obstruction to the work of the last Independence 
Delegation and for high treason to the ideal of the Filipino People. 
At least that would seem to be true in the case of Osmeha, they say. 

Among the comments made says: "If Quezon has campaigned 
in the second senatorial district against Osias, is now campaigning 
in the fourth senatorial district against Palma, and will go to Capiz 
to fight Roxas, what is the reason for not coming to Cebu to fight 
the supreme head of the Pros?”

“Voice of i\;ople I 
Urged Me to Run,” 
Diaz Says in Capiz 

Special to the TRIBUNE 

Capiz, May 18. — delegation of about 1,000 from the first district ^ 
greeted ex-Judge Diaz upon his arrival in Capiz this afternoon. 

There was a torch light parade' on the principal streets of the town/g# 
The paraders were wild with enthusiasm shouting all the way ihrough;^^ 
“Viva Antis, “ “Down Pros,” “Down with Roxas,” /‘Down with Dynas- ^ 
ty,” and^'Y^va Diaz.” • , 

About 2,000 heard Messrs. Diaz He declared that he decided to 
and Hontiveros speak. In his I support the Quezon leadership be- 



speech, ex-Judge Diaz defended 'the 
judiciary, saying tlmt it is inde- 
pendent from politics. He also de- 
clared that he became a candidate, 
because it is the voice, of the peo- 
ple that urges him to run. 

He told the people that the antis 
will carry the coming election 
throughout the islands with an im- 
mense majority as told to him by 
President Quezon' in both the 
house and the senate. 



cause it is sound, telling the peo- 
ple that the national issue now i 
is the leadership between Quezon ;| 
and the Osrox group. 

Ex-Judge Hpntivero.s pointed to ' 
the people to blame the economic 
provi.slons of the H-H-C law as 
well as. the Tydings-McDuffie law 
on the pros. The Osrox mission 
was a factor in the death of the 
H-HC law, hence Mr. Quezon, he 
said, cannot be blamed.

^st night, the anti meeting held at

most applauded of the orators

Party candidate for representative 
Of the south district. Mr. Mendo- 
za spoke of the present education 
of the youths, bewailing the fact 
that high school graduates know 
the life histories of great Ameri* 
cans, but are entirely shamefully 
ignorant of the lives of their own 
heroes.

nal system, Mr. Mencioza went on 
to speali about the Tydings-Mc- 






Duffie d.nd tr.e ja,-* xho.

from the HHC law because it 1

dence. whll© the HHC law did not'

tainty.

ways atacked him at their meetr

merly an enemy of President 
Quezon he Is now one of his 
slaves . The reason for his join- 
ing forces with President Quezon ’ 
he explained, is that he and his 
party has always believed that 
I the HHc law did not promise real 
independence to the Islands. Hj 
challei^ed his opponent, Repre^ 
sentaUvo R.emiglo, to a public de- 
bate.

Mabuting Pagkakataon 



Pagkaraan ng ilang araw ua pagkakaratay dahil 
sa isang karamdaman, ay ipinahayag ng mga mangga- 
gamot ng pangulong Mamxel - Quezon na maaari na si- 
yang maglakbay, kaya ngayong umaga’y tutulak siya 
ua patungong Iloilo, Kapis at Antike, upang ibandila 
’ roon ang simulain ng mga anti. 

Ikinalulugod namin at magkakaroon ng pagkaka- 
taon’ ang patnugot-bansa na mailahad sa Kaiimugan 
ang buong katotohanan sa kalagayan ng ating usapin 
at ng mga suliraning pangkasalukuyan, pagka’t marl- ; 
rinig at mababatid ng mga lalawigang sasadyaln niya 
ang mga tunay na pangyayaring tinatalimwang ng 
mga Osrox. Samantalang nasa Estados Unidos ang 
Misyon Quezon at hanggang nang matapos na tangga- 
; pin ng Lehislatui’a ang uwi niyang batas ng kasarin- 
lan, ay wala nang pinag-ubusan ng panahon ang mga 
Osmena, Roxas at Montinola, kundi iianim sa isip ng 
mga mamamayan ang marurumiug binhi ng kasinu- 
ngalingan, lagyan ng piring ang mata ng mga niangha- 
halal upang di makilala ang puti’t ang.itim ,at akayin 
sila sa mga patibong sa pulitika upang masamantala 
^ halalan -sa ika-5 ng Hunyo. Ang lahat ng ito’y ka- 
ilangang ipamulat a.t liwanagin. 

I Si G. Quezon ay patutungo sa Kabisayaan sa kapa- 
. kanan ng isang simulain. Hindi siya darayo roon sa 
kaibigan lamang ng kanyang sarili, kundi. upang tumu- 
gon sa walang patlang na kahilingan ng mga mama- 
mayan na linavvin sa kanila ang mga pang-unang suli- 
raning nilabusaw ng mga mapaglubid ng buhangin. Da- 
la nga ng lider sa mga taga-timog ang isang dakilang 
hangarin, at ito’y ang iliglas sila sa kamay ng mga 
pulitikong hindi nagtatapat sa bayan.

Cebu, Cebu, May 23.— The ban- ' 
quet which will be given in honor ' 
Of Senate President Manuel L». 
Quezon and his delegation who are 
' expected to arrive in Cebu, in the 
. course of his tour of the southern 
islands will be held in the cargo * 
shed of Pier 2. • ~ 

The pier was selected by the ' 
committee as it is the only place ! 
which can hold the 3,000 scats 
which have been sold for the ban- 
, quet . 

Senator Sergio Osnieh^ and his 
party will be given preferred seats 
despite the fact that the organiz- 
ers belong to the opposite fac- 
tion. All the 52 towns of the prov- 
ince and the neighboring provinces 
will be represented. Banners on 
which are inscribed the names of 
the towns represented will be dis- 
played . 

President Quezon has informed 
■Governor M. Jesus Cuenco of his 
larrival in Cebu during the week. I 
A hymn to be sung by 10 voices! 
will be featured during the wel-^ 
^comc ceremonies. j

The Basis In 
Economic Theory 



'' ^ Presiden t Quezon’s opposition to tariff le/rislation wliiclv 
will increase the cost of living in these islands without ««•'' 

• < ' compensating advantaged ' 

the long run to the people, ha^ 
ample support in economic 
theoiy. 

The government uses the 

» tariff as an instrument fof 
creating a direct benefit for its nationals. The tariff legi^** 
lation which President Quezon had in mind, in voicing liis 
opposition, would not tally with this fundamental use of the 
tariff. And this is so because the Philippines is .still an un- 
develojiefl country; and its economic life is not a single sys- 
tem of specialization within which the application of tariff 
legislation will redound to the benefit of the people. 

Under such a .system, higher tariff rates which are held 
to increase the prices of commodities to the consumers, may 
not have that result because the diffei’ence, upward, in the 
rates could be absorbed by lai'ger investments. But it is 
useless to hope for that efficiency of larger investments in 
a country still undeveloped like ours. 

The mutuality of benefit in a continued trade relation 
between the United. States and the Philippines, the states- 
manship of the American leaders and of President Quezo^ 
will have to square with economic theory that it be foi' 
permanent welfare of both nations.

WASHINGTON. D.C. April 12,— 
Senate President Manuel L. Quezon 
announced that he is remaining; in 
Washington for further conversa- 
tions with President Roosevelt and 
Governor General Murphy. 

Sen. Claro M. Recto and Repre- 
sentative Manuel Roxas are leaving 
for Manila on Monday. They will 
sail on the S. S. President Hoover 
from San Fiancisco on April 19.

ference. with Roosevelt which was 
accepted as an indications of the 
widening of the scope of tl>.e Philg

20 Years Ago— April 25, 1015 

Japan again handed another ul- 
timatum to China lor the latter^ 
to either accede to her 21 demands 
or be prepared for war, giving - 
China a period of three days to 
make up her mind. Throughout 
Nippon this day, wrote the chro- 
nicler, the people wore restless, 
holding indignant demonstrations 
and urging the goyornment to take 
drastic action against- China. Ja- 
panese l>rcss iiterally boiled with 
editorials denouncing Count Oku- 
ma, the Incumbent premier, for be- 
ing’ too lenient and for not pur- 
suing a policy which was commen- 
surate with the dignity and honor 
of the empire. 

It was in this condition that Re- 
sident Commissioner M ati uel U 
Quezon then returning to his native 
heath following a trumphant cam- 
nalgn for the Jones bill arrived in 
Tanan. The chronicler also re- 
corded that Don Manuel was rc-- 
ceived by the Mikado in audience.

LA DEJICULTURA 



E l culto Dr. Pardo de Tavera, en una charla muy inte- 
resante y amena repleta de observaciones que did en 
yida hace cerca de 20 anos en el “Philippine Columbian” en 
Pasay, donde estuvimos de oyentes, hizo esta notable afir- 
macion: “La indu.stria del coco no es agricultura, es deji-. 

cultura”. ,iPor que? Por que el cocalero, despues de .sem-i 
brar los arboles de coco, deja que las frutas caigan por si' 
solas, 0 hace que otros las descuelguen. ! 

Posiblemente no sea esta la razon por la cual niiestros j 
cocaleros no ban estado nunca organizados, y no cuenten 
con abundantes recursos para la defensa de sus intereses. 
Son “dejicultores”; pero, la queja del mismo, Presidente Que- 
zon de que nuestro gobierno se ha mostrado muy tarclio en 
enviar su protesta contra la ley de California que castiga al 
aceite vegetal filipino, justifica hasta cierto punto la obser- 
vacion del difunto Dr. Pardo de Tavera, de que en esto de la^ 
industria del coco hoy mucho de “dejicultura . j

By United iPress 
WASHINGTON, Appil 4 (By 
Mail) . — . Filipinos accepted the 
Tydings-McDuffie Act in the 
hope that later its provisions 
would be ameUorated, President 
of the Senate . Alanuel L. .Quezon 
told the House Insular ' Affairs 
Committee today. 

Speaking at the cordage hear- 
ing, Quezon, made the fol- 
lowing explanation, of how the 
HareiHawes-Cutting Act was re- 
jected and the Tydings-McDuffie 

Act accepted; 

fought the' Hare-Hawes-Cut. 
ting Act. I did not- think that it 
was fair: I was sent by the leg- 

islature to the United States- to see 
if we could get a better law. I 
succeeded in getting the military 
provisions eliminated. 

*<Whon I tried to^get improved 
trade relations, I was told that if 
I attempted, to improve the trade 
relations as provided in the law, 1 
would - probably get something 
worse. Then as a good practical 
man I shut my mouth. 

“Then I saw ’the ‘President, and 
told him that my people were no_t 
datisfled, . but they ‘ Would ^accept ; 
thl3 law’ in the hope that when the 
i economic situation in' the United 
States improved, and it was real- 
ized over here that it wae advan- 
tageous to the United States %kj 
have prosperity in the Philippines, 
that we would then be able to re- 
vise this law and improve it. 1 
don't know if this is what the Pre- - 
sideut had ‘ in mind when he sent 
his message to Congress referring 
to possibmtles of improving the 



law later.

Toyabas Sakdalistas Claim They Are 
Persecution Victims in Plea to G. G. 



1 A 

Sakdalistas orf Tayabas whos'e 
colleagues are accuse^ of planning 
to assassinate* in a 

letter alleging grievances, have ap- 
pealed to the governor general for 
executive intervention. 

The signers of the petition claim 
that they are candidates who op- 
posed thos’e of Mr. Quezon in the 
last election, that they propose to 
vote against th© ratification of the 
Constitution, that it is in an ef- 
fort to restrain them in the ex- 
ercise of their right to vote that 
they are threatened with prosecu- 
tion for .sedition, as in fact some 
of them have already been ac- 
cused of the same. 

The petitioners state further: 

“If any of us transcend the li- 
mits of the law, we believe there 
are hundreds of prosecuting of- 
ficers and thousands of constabu- 
lary men to prosecute and punish 
him. But the right to denounce 
such abuses in broad day-light In- 
stead of conducting a whispering 
capaign, and io express our will 
as to the approval or disapproval 
of the Constitution, we believe, 
cannot be suppressed on the pre- 
text of safeguarding public order. 
That has been always and every- 
v/here the pretext for political and 
personal despotism; the excuse for 
the hated previous censure. We 
1 have had tasted it during the 
1 Spanish regime. In fact that pur- 



pose of said authorities In prevent- 
ing our meetings and in falsely ■ 
prosecuting us. is to perpetuate 
theit' abuses even at the cost of 
our economic and political misery. 
They are the ones who should bo 
prosecuted for conspiring to incite 
sedition.

The state burial of Associate 
Justice Pedro Concepcion, ori- 
ginally scheduled for this af*. 
ternoon, will be held on Sun- 
day morning at La Loma ceme- 
tery, it was decided yesterdhy 
by the committee, headed by 
Secretary of Justice* Jose Abad 
Santos, named by President 

Manuel L. jQuezon to take 
charge of the funeral arrange- 
ments. 

Services will be held at the 
session hall of the supreme 
court in the Ayuntamiento at 
8 a.m. on Sunday, and intex'- 
ment will take place immedi- 
ately after the services. 

The body, which is now lying* 

in state at the Paz Funeral Par- • 
•s ^i^lorg on Misericordia, will be trans- 

forred to the Ayuntamiento on 
^ w Saturday afternoon. 

Justice Concepcion, who retir- 
ed only recently as chairman of 
the commisison on elections, died 
suddenly last Sunday morning at 
the family home in Antipolo, Ki- 
ln accordance with a resolution 
passed yesterday morning by the 
iudges of the Manila court of 
-first instance all flags of court 
buildings will be flown at half- 
mast beginning today until the 
burial of the late Justice Concep- 
cion. * , , 

The judges also passed a resolu- 

tion of condolence on the death 
of the eminent jurist. The resolu- 
tion will be presented to Mrs. 
Concepcion by a committee com- 
posed of Judge Gervaslo Diaz, 
chairman, and Judges Mamerto 
Roxas and Vicente* Albert, mem- 
. . The resolution recalled that 

T.iatice Concepcion “displayed 
Sng the entire period of his in- 
cumbency a lofty sense of justice, 
broad tolerance, and strict devo- 
tion to duty. , « 

As a further show of respect 

for 'the late member of the su- 
preme court, the judges also de- 
cided to cancel all trials on the 
,i«te of his interment, to attend 
the funeral, and to send floral 
offerings.

‘^THE OPPOSITION ABORNING

gathering on the political horizon to themselves, much less a political

constructive opposition party is to Iook prospects of an opposition party headed by 



j. ^ X c/ j-^1 ui an opposition party neacieci ny 

for' the impossible. There are spots General Aguinaldo. But now those pros- 
of various colors — running largely to pects have faded in thin air. After weeks 
- ' "1 -but to harmonize these hesitation, marked by false starts and 



shades of red- vw -j ^ ^ - 

into a picture that makes any sense is out as a com- 

, X? n . monwealth Presidential candidate but with- 

and commands a following would re- out a party around him or a platform under 
quire a master of political manipula- him. His announcement of candidacy was 



1 




tioii. First of all it would call for a far 
better leadership than yet has showed 
up on the camp of any of the so-called 
radical groups. 

There has to be an undercurrent 
of unrest and a breakdown in respect 
for the functions and functionaries of 
government for the situation to reach 
its present stage. That unrest and loss 
of regard for constituted authority af- 
ford working grounds for new political 
doctrines is a fact that no one can deny. 
But the formation of an effective 
opposition party calls for more than 
mere destructive defiance. The swing 
toward radicalism thus far noticeable 
offers nothing constructive. In its 
destructiveness the movement has dis- 
credited itself legally. The whimper- 
ings of the riot-and-run leaders who ^ 
take to the hills and hide themselves 
in jungles and caves when they see the 
spilled blood of their ignorant arid gul- 
, lible- 'followers whom they push — riot 
- lead -— into revolutionary battle belies 
any_ claim of a courageous or cOnstruc- 
! tive leadership. 

i • now has dropped below 

, anydiing that could even be called a respect- 
able revolution. There are two lines of ' 
activity noW, sniping for prosecuting wit- 
nesses on the one .side and chasine* rem- 



primarily a call for personal following, 
hardly a challenge to Uin leade rship, i 

although it long has been a recognized fact; 
that opposition to the Quezon leadership 
was the .substance from which the Agui- 
naldo platform was to be constructed. 

The law abiding spirit of General Agui- 
naldo, his' large property owning status and 
the general set-up of his political organi- 
zation together uith his many declarations 
of respect for constituted authority make it 
virtually impossible for him and his plat- 
formless group to become the nucleus of any 
radical opposition party. There is too much 
difference between the atmospheres of Sak- 
dal Cabuyao and Aguinaldo Kawit to blend 
them into the life breath of an effective 
and/or constructive opposition party. 

To give an opposition the strength to 
make it histing and politically formidable 
the spirit behind it must go beyond the stage 
of mere criticism. An effective opposition 
must be constructive, not merely destruc- 
tive. It must not merely shout vicious 
charges at the existing leadei*.ship and the 
constituted government. It must not merely 
call Quezon names. To justify its existence 
and to win support it must advance con- 
structive remedies for those things it con- 
demns. 

Any government remedy to be construe-' 
tively effective must have as a part of it 
financing facilities. Much of the criticism 
lately to be heard ties itself into knots. The 
professional agitators would have the peo- 
ple think that it is possible to abolish taxes 
and at the same time deal generously in the 
distribution of governmental appropriations, 
or in other words to stop the money from 
coming in and at the same time run it out 
in bigger streams. They would kill the trade 
which gives the government its daily bread- 
and at the same time expect bigger feasts 
for the masses of the people.

President and Mrs. Quezon And 
Archbishop to Attend Inauguration 



The inaugamtion Ai the reconstructed church of Cabiao, Nueva 
Ecija will take place today and tomorrow with representatives from 
•>gh official, ecclesiastical and social circles, headed by President Ma- 
His Grace, Most Rev, Michael .L .O’Doherty, Archbishop 
^nila, and Mrs. Quezon, attending. . 

The work of reconstruction began in April last year under e 
airect.on of the Rev. Father Sotero Martin, who was assisted by a 
group of friends. including Assemblyman and Mrs. Felipe Buen^mino, 
1 • Funds for the work were obtained through popular sub.scriptlons. 
the new church is built along California Mission lines, and is the only 
of its kind in the Islands. 



■f "At 5 o’clock this evenii^ the' 
’guests will assemble for the bless- 
ing of .the church. His Grace 
will officiate. Tomorrow morning 
at 6 o’clock there will be general 
communion, to be followed by pon- 
tifical high mass at 7 o’clock. His 
Grace will officiate, assisted by the 
seminarists of San Carlos Semin- 
ary Manila. The Rev. Father Nl- 
can’or dc Guzman will give the 
sermon. Choral music will be sup- 
plied by the San Carlos Seminary. 
At noon a luncheon will be given 
bv the members of the Catholic 
Action. 

President and Mrs, Quezon are 
sponsors for the main altar, while 
the following well-known Catholic 
ladies are patronesser. for the cole- i

THE MASONIC HOME, ^SCHOOL AND DORMITORY 








By LEO FISCHER 

The biggest fund in the posses<;inn 

Lodge of Free and Accepted Masons of the PhiifSe 
Islands is what is now known as thp Mol 
School and Dormitory Fund or to ?“r' 

title, the Home Fund For this reL™ o, d t®’’'’-*' f 
in view o£ the fact that from time to time dSes 
on the use and investment of this Fund arise on 
floor of the Grand Lodge or elsewhere and no one 

and grew, by 



what regulations it is governed, and how it is invested. 



we have compiled what useful information concerning 
it we have been able to find in Grand Lodge Proceed- 
ings, The Cabletow, and other sources, and submit it 
to the Cl aft in order to make these data available to 
our Brethren who have not the opportunitv or leisure 
to consult the original sources. 

The Masonic Home Fund was created a little over 
seventeen years ago, when the world was still en- 
gaged in the Great War. It was in January, 1918, at 
the Sixth Annual Communication of the Grand Lodge 
of the Philippine Islands, that M. W. Bro. Newton C. 
Comfoi’t, Grand Secretary, recommended in his report 
to the Grand Lodge (1918 Proceedings, p. 45) “That 
the founding of a Widows’ and Orphans’ Home and 
School, together with a Dispensary '.i^r our Members, 
be referred to a Committee of Ways and Means to be 
known as the Masonic Home Board, with instructions 
to hold regular public meetings to study the situation 
and make as complete a report as practicable to Grand 
Lodge next year.” 

In accordance with this recommendation, the 
incoming Grand Master, M. W. Bro. Manuel L. Quezon, 
appointed the first Masonic Home Board," croTISiSting 
of himself and Bros. Milton E. Springer, Rafael 
Palma, Tomas Earnshaw, Walter R. Macfarlane, 
Felipe Buencamino, Jr., and Jose McMicking. 

At the same Communication of the Grand Lodge, 
the Committee on Finance, consisting of Bros. Manuel 
Paredes and W. R; Macfarlane, recommended that of 
the P4,000 on hand over and above the year’s expenses, 
P2,000 be constituted into a Fund to be known as the 
Widows’ and Orphans’ Home and School Fund. After 
various motions and considerable debate, Bro. Salva- 
vador Chofre’s motion, seconded by Bro. Walter A. 
Smith, “That the entire four thousand pesos be invest- 
ed and be constituted into the fund recommended by 
the Committee,” was carried without pPP?**’*^**^”' 

At the Seventh Annual Communication (1919) , , 
Deputy Grand Master Milton E. Springer reported that 
the Board had held several meetings, “but no definite 
action was taken, as it was decided to give all oui 
energies and finances to aid in winning the ixiea 
War.” 

With the interest from the investmei^ (Masonic 
Temple As.sociation 7% bonds), the Fund then 
amounted to P4,062.00, and tlie 
recommended that the sum of P2.000 be trans 
from the General Fund to the Home Fund, whicn 

recommendation was approved. 

The Home Board appointed by tbe i^^mnu 

Grand Master was headed by M. W. Bio. 

Springer,

Jiist Anther Show In Town 

Coalition! That’s the noliH^oi'T T~ 
shop, political gossipers’ rendezvous a barber 

canang lobby, anywhere: GoaliH^n • market, in the Maln^ 
debates, Coalition is always the subient^ topic. In public 

and that party talk of it: in leaders talk of it; this 



timT ‘3 wasting 

inrmri political innovation 

ncroduced by a certain U. S. Ex-Se- 

,^“^/e«dily embraced b^^our 
ational leaders, no less than our^ A-1 
statesman and Statistician - The 
yuezmi-Osmena oligarchy,” to 

quote the Suuiulong broadside^ 

As if the thing Coalition is strange 
o U9^ we find ourselves divided ag- 
am into two opposing camps: the 
pro and anti coalitionists. One shouts 
that the turkey served is stale and 
tool injurious to our national health, 
while the other maintains it is fresh 
'ind beneficial to our national growth. 

hich of the two is right, one can ne- 
vera uthoritatively adduce. Both have 
their grounds to stand pat. 

But 1 have my own idea of what 
this Coalition business is. To me it 
is nothing but a political gesture, 

^ just another show in town. Like the 

field of moviedom, the political horizon is limitless, affording di- 
rectors and scenario writers inexhaustible subjects for the next show. 
We have hsd the H-H-C scramble, the T-M wrangle, resulting in 
the parting of the ways of our Osmefia and Quezon, and we have 
had many allied political shows which did nothing but pollute the air 
with stingy names. It created a storm after which the proverbial 
calmness followed. Unfortunately, however, people are naturally 
jovial, sensation and thrill lovers, and it is no wonder that they 
find hullabaloos again in the COALITION pie which is how stirring 
Juan de la Cruz. It is simply just another show in town which will 
entertain you and me. 

Coalition or no Coalition, our national problems remain the 
same. Without Coalition, the political parties, whether it be a Ma- 
jority or a Minority, work for all that is best for its country’s inte- 
rest. With Coalition, juat the same the country’s interest is above 
all consideration. You may have reasons for the Coalition, yet ^^9" 
ther Juan has his too. And mind ;^ou, dear readers, whatever their 
reasonings are, both are striving for the best interest of their coun- 
try. Isn’t the present sensation just another show in town? er- 
tainly it is a political gesture Q.E. D. -

Chamber Head 

Presicienj^uezon assures Presid- 
ent L. R. ^mnsmo ’tSrthe cham- 
ber of Commerce of the Philippine 
Islands of his cooperation with the 
chamber in a message 'Wired direct-

in reply to a message of congra- 
tulation sent to him and to Gover- 
nor Murphy by Mr. Aguinaldo. The 
reply was received in the office of 
Mr. Aguinaldo and reads as fol- 
lows: 

“Mr. L. R. Aguinaldo, 

Pres., Chamber of Commerce 
of the Philippine Islands. 

I am deeply grateful for your 
message and I wish to assure you 
of my cooperation with the Cham- 
ber of 'Commerce of the Philippine 
Islands. 

Quezon.” 

The message sent by president 
Aguinaldo was of congratulation 
for.the splendid work done by Pres. 
Quezon and Governor General 
Murphy and other members of the 
Quezon mission for the speedy ap- 
proval by President Roosevelt of 
the Philippine constitution, exprossl 
ing deep appreciation on behalf of 
the chamber.

IN, METALLURGY, *ho fusion of a metal with 
mother metal, or of a metal with a non-metal, produces 
in aPoy. Stretchini? ihe analei>y into the Philippine po- 
litical realm, the fusion of the “Pro” and the “Anti” 
jartics, v/hich is the new. proposal oy a few political 
svisf^'i^res. will likely produce what might be termed a 
political alloy. 

It is an open secret that the majority party counts 
with many members who abhor the nronosed coalition, 
as they would a pest. Proponents of the newfangled 
“fusion” of the two major entities hope by this scheme 
to purge out those very members who refuse to be melt- 
ed into one political organization. 

On the other hand, the new proposal may turn the 
general watchful waiting of many minority men liito a 
mounting dislike" ami suspicion. To explain this proba- 
bility, we have to go clown into a primal law of nature 
where the larger substance blots out the identity of the 
lesser when they are mixed together. ...

of the legislature, scheduled

business is finished, it was’ 
announced this morning by 
President Q|ue2on . The re- 
"port that the 'fecial and re- 

gular sessions have heeh combin-i 
ed came as ai surprise in view of! 
the opposition to the plan voiced! 
j by Governor General Frank Mur- 
phy. 

I The decision to continue the 
1 session, It is understood, was 
reached at an 11th hour confer- 
ence, wherein the chief executive 
was convinced by the reasons ad 
vanced by the leaders that it 
would be more expedient, and 
more economical in both time 
and money if the two sessions 
were combined. In view of this 
decision, the leaders are now very 
btisy rushing up plans for the 
lengthy session, including a pro- 
gram of legislation. 

With the combination of the 
sessions, ithe legislature will no 
longer meet in usual regular ses- 
sion. Its next session will be held 
for the purpose of canvassing the 
iresults of the Commonwealth elec-* 
tions, after which it will be dis-* 
solved to give way to the Assem- :
